<div id="review_1272061606" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1272061606" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1272061606?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781593083168" href="/book/show/640052.Villette">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=vintage">vintage</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=women-and-books">women-and-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=mesmerizing">mesmerizing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1272061606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3957710339994027076"><i>
  <blockquote>No mockery in this world ever sounds to me so hollow as that of being told to cultivate happiness. What does such advice mean? Happiness is not a potato, to be planted in mould, and tilled with manure. Happiness is a glory shining far down upon us out of Heaven. She is a divine dew which the soul, on certain of its summer mornings, feels dropping upon it from the amaranth bloom and golden fruitage of Paradise.</blockquote>
</i><br/>I love when this paradoxical life brings me a book laced with "composite and contra</span>
  <span id="freeText3957710339994027076" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>No mockery in this world ever sounds to me so hollow as that of being told to cultivate happiness. What does such advice mean? Happiness is not a potato, to be planted in mould, and tilled with manure. Happiness is a glory shining far down upon us out of Heaven. She is a divine dew which the soul, on certain of its summer mornings, feels dropping upon it from the amaranth bloom and golden fruitage of Paradise.</blockquote>
</i><br>I love when this paradoxical life brings me a book laced with "composite and contracted" meaning, one of philosophical ponder and pathos; one wherein solitude grasps for Hope in order to avoid Despair and longing is elucidated and layered. Dreamlike and peculiar at times, a revelation of inner thought, the reflective narrative never ceases to make its reader consider life and its oddities, life and its happiness and pain. At a time in my life when I'm at a crossroads with two interesting professional decisions that were forced upon me by this <i>life</i>, I am humbled that <i>Villette</i> occupied my still moments.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1495222529i/22797893._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This is the story of what happens when a woman finds herself in the midst of a strange community, with aloof, pretentious, and judgmental people; when she must ground herself in an academic environment that overflows with pretenses and mockery. This novel's trajectory is what happens when love is unrequited, for it demands social status from the one it inhabits. These three meandering volumes make lucid the loneliness that blooms within, one that stems from loss of family and identity. <br><br>I smiled when I read Bronte's explanation for Lucy Snowe because while I loved Polly, I had a deep admiration for Lucy's steadfastness and professional journey, and I also admired Charlotte Brontë who made no apologies for her character. Here is how Brontë describes Lucy in a letter:<br><i>
  <blockquote>You say that she may be thought morbid and weak, unless the history of her life be more fully given. I consider that she is both morbid and weak at times; her character sets up no pretensions to unmixed strength, and anybody living her life would necessarily become morbid.</blockquote>
</i><br>And in a letter to her publisher Brontë wrote: "I greatly apprehend, however, that the weakest character in the book is the one I aimed at making the most beautiful..."<br><br>I never really did understand, and in fact detested, Paul Emmanuel's patronizing ways in the first part of the novel. It was interesting to learn that he stands in for Constantin Heger, the husband of the school director that Brontë worked for. Graham, however, seems like a stud - caring, kind, generous, intelligent - and it is easy to see how Lucy would have fallen in love with him. However, to say much about plot would 'spoil' the story and mislead the reader, for this narrative is an extension of memory, a sequence of consciousness that occurs through contemplation and reflection, which makes the plot both surprising and revealing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3957710339994027076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2185481727" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2185481727" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/202724-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601676054p2/202724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2185481727?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/202724-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18619526-villette">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=theclassics">theclassics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=educational-challenge-2014">educational-challenge-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2185481727" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4935051990970440369">I finished this last night and I'm STILL ANGRY.<br/><br/>WHAT THE HELL, CHARLOTTE?<br/><br/>I mean, seriously. I would also like to sit down with the person who wrote the introduction and talked about how Villette is so much better than Jane Eyre. I would like to speak to this person about their drug habit, and how it's affecting their work performance. Because . . . WHAT . . . did I just read? And WHY have so many of my friends given this book 5 stars?<br/><br/>Now, as some of you may know, I love Jane Eyre. I mean, I LOVE </span>
  <span id="freeText4935051990970440369" style="display:none">I finished this last night and I'm STILL ANGRY.<br /><br />WHAT THE HELL, CHARLOTTE?<br /><br />I mean, seriously. I would also like to sit down with the person who wrote the introduction and talked about how Villette is so much better than Jane Eyre. I would like to speak to this person about their drug habit, and how it's affecting their work performance. Because . . . WHAT . . . did I just read? And WHY have so many of my friends given this book 5 stars?<br /><br />Now, as some of you may know, I love Jane Eyre. I mean, I LOVE JANE EYRE. It is without a doubt in my top ten books of all time. And I love it not because of the romance, but because I love Jane. Jane is not afraid to speak her mind. Jane is not afraid to seek out love. Jane is not afraid to say, I respect myself too damn much to be your mistress, even though you are a sexy beast and I want you. Jane is an artist. Jane is a loyal friend. Jane is amazeballs.<br /><br />Villette is about Lucy Snowe. Lucy Snowe doesn't talk a lot. Years worth of stuff happens to her and she goes, Meh, well, that was a thing. Lucy is easily irritated by people, and enjoys being alone (which I did appreciate), and Lucy is much put upon by people who sort of use her and abuse her, take advantage of her retiring nature, send her letters and buy her gowns when they remember her, drop her when they are busy with other people. Lucy likes walking around in gardens, and she's fine. Okay, sure. I was okay with all of this. It wasn't better than Jane Eyre, but it was okay.<br /><br />I was okay with it right up until she starts tearing out her hair and flinging herself around sobbing because a guy who has been a COMPLETE ASSHOLE to her for the last 400 pages is going away. A guy who constantly harps on her clothes, and tells her that she should wear dull colors and no jewelry because she isn't meant for such things. A guy who insults her intelligence, treats her like a child or a pet, spies on her, steals from her, mocks her in public. A guy who rages at her and calls her a slut for exchanging letters with a male friend. <br /><br />No. Just no. This man is the most horrible of all the horrible people who surround Lucy, and I am extremely upset that she didn't tell him not to let the door hit him on his (badly dressed, cigar-smelling) ass as he left. <br /><br />GRRR.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4935051990970440369" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_412697024" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/412697024" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/412697024?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/16168389-villette">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=books-are-the-best-invention">books-are-the-best-invention</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2013">r-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=cross-my-heart-and-hope-to-die">cross-my-heart-and-hope-to-die</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=virago">virago</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer412697024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2027711972349612793">We denizens of 'The Book of Disquiet' salute you.<br/><br/>We of the small loves and small livings, the tiny joys and tiny dreams, bid you welcome. Our home is well-adjusted and self-assured, for if we profess ourselves any sort of connoisseur, it lies within those realms. Our work keeps us fed, clothed, ticking along at a methodical pace that matches the step of our action.<br/><br/>Our doings are wrested from the very root of us, and we cannot remember a time when our will was a creature without chain or muzzle.<br/><br/>W</span>
  <span id="freeText2027711972349612793" style="display:none">We denizens of 'The Book of Disquiet' salute you.<br /><br />We of the small loves and small livings, the tiny joys and tiny dreams, bid you welcome. Our home is well-adjusted and self-assured, for if we profess ourselves any sort of connoisseur, it lies within those realms. Our work keeps us fed, clothed, ticking along at a methodical pace that matches the step of our action.<br /><br />Our doings are wrested from the very root of us, and we cannot remember a time when our will was a creature without chain or muzzle.<br /><br />We of the thoughtful posing and quiet undertaking, the nondescript manner and stoic expression, pass you by. Our persona is mature and respectable, for if we claim ourselves any manner of actor, in those appearances we reign supreme. Our countenance keeps us from harm, trouble, the majority of unwelcome intrusions and unexpected disturbances.<br /><br />Our face once feared the cruel judgment of every eye, and we will never know how much we have lost in maintaining its proud coldness.<br /><br />We of the reticent life and withdrawn days, the slow solitude and meandering existence, pray you keep at a distance. Our existence is of much self and little other, for if we must cluster our many sensibilities under a single roof, we will choose a room of our own. Our self-appraisals keep us safe, secure, a well measured freedom in the functions of a perfectly plotted daily life.<br /><br />Our souls cry, and cry, and cry, for we have not yet found the permanent satisfaction that such an existence promises.<br /><br />We of the careful cravings and hesitant urges, the hard won realizations and fierce practices, present to you on rare occasions. Our passions are few and foremost, for if we believe ourselves the bearer of any kind of talent, we cling to it as a ballast of temporal assurance. Our works keep us a measure of the past, future, a present that without such doings would slip into the void of useless persistence.<br /><br />Our praxis heeds neither standard nor accreditation, and thus we are admired, and thus we are condemned.<br /><br />We of the observant eye and sardonic grin, the quickening wit and sober analysis, say to you, beware! Our modus operandi is an invisible seething, for if we name our most finely tuned instinct, it is the instantaneous measure of irony of any and all. Our entertainment keeps us amused in parts, and fully familiarized with the discordant pomposity of reality in others.<br /><br />Ignorance is bliss, a garden from which we were banished long ago, forevermore to discontentedly mock and claw ourselves bloody on our own eternal hypocrisies.<br /><br />We of the accumulated being and carved out philosophy, the chaotic incorporations and weathered discombobulations, forbid you the ease of category. Our mind is our own and ours alone, for if we hold ourselves to any creed, we demand it change with our every breath and drop of blood. Our sustenance keep us alive, and woe to any who choose only between spitting us out and swallowing us whole.<br /><br />It is lonely, here, but nowhere else will let us be.<br /><br />We of the experienced heart and cautious brain, the creeping desire and subtle attractions, set you at a distance. Our love knows itself very well, for if there is one thing it characterizes itself by, it is the painfully slow and all encompassing spread of loyalty incarnate. Our self very rarely finds another it can devote itself to, and knows itself too tightly reined to come to any foolish end.<br /><br />We bury our seeds too deeply, and their strangling growths are doomed to die without a trace of reciprocating sun.<br /><br />And so, we denizens of 'Villette' bid you adieu. We are a small, strange, and sad sort, and our weirdly warped self-censures are likely to accrue as life goes on. Much more likely to build up into an age old oubliette within which we quietly fade to our own ends, than to erode. However, if you are patient, and you do care, we may come out again. We take long in developing affection, and even longer in feeling confident to bestow such affections unlooked for, but if you seek us out and encourage from us the same, who knows. We will still be mindful of all the rest, but perhaps, yes. We will come out to play.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2027711972349612793" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_44754601" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44754601" />
    <a title="The Book Whisperer (aka Boof)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1253478-the-book-whisperer-aka-boof"><img alt="The Book Whisperer (aka Boof)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1268256340p2/1253478.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44754601?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="The Book Whisperer (aka Boof)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="The Book Whisperer (aka Boof)" href="/user/show/1253478-the-book-whisperer-aka-boof">The Book Whisperer (aka Boof)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199536658" href="/book/show/3166422-villette">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1253478-the-book-whisperer-aka-boof?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1253478-the-book-whisperer-aka-boof?shelf=victorians">victorians</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1253478-the-book-whisperer-aka-boof?shelf=uk">uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1253478-the-book-whisperer-aka-boof?shelf=my-most-amazing-books">my-most-amazing-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1253478-the-book-whisperer-aka-boof?shelf=british-authors">british-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1253478-the-book-whisperer-aka-boof?shelf=read-in-2009">read-in-2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1253478-the-book-whisperer-aka-boof?shelf=comfort-reading">comfort-reading</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44754601" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14442746731080880861">Reader, I heart Ms. Bronte! Reading <i> Villette </i> was like reading a huge epic that I was so emmersed in that I walked in Lucy Snowe's shoes, I felt what she felt. How many authors can do that to you?<br/><br/>Lucy Snowe is difficult to get to know at first. In fact, she is difficult to like. This is deliberate; she tells you about other people, what they think, what they feel, but precious little about herself, of whom she appears fiercely private. Only as the story unfolds does she start to let you in - I </span>
  <span id="freeText14442746731080880861" style="display:none">Reader, I heart Ms. Bronte! Reading <i> Villette </i> was like reading a huge epic that I was so emmersed in that I walked in Lucy Snowe's shoes, I felt what she felt. How many authors can do that to you?<br /><br />Lucy Snowe is difficult to get to know at first. In fact, she is difficult to like. This is deliberate; she tells you about other people, what they think, what they feel, but precious little about herself, of whom she appears fiercely private. Only as the story unfolds does she start to let you in - I remember being surprised when she showed such tender, gentle thoughts and actions towards the sick daughter of her employer; that, I believe, was the first glimpse of emotion from Lucy and it really endeared me to her. Lucy Snowe's name was not an accident - Bronte toyed with Lucy Frost for a while before settling on Snowe. She also allows us to see her as others do: <i> "Crabbed and crusty" </i> said Ginevra, a pupil at the school, and <i> "unfeeling thing that I was" </i> written to her in a letter. The point is, she isn't unfeeling at all. She is lonely and trying to make her way in an unfamiliar world. Lucy's past is only hinted at but it appears to have been an unhappy one. <br /><br />Brontes prose is gorgeous, <i> Villette </i> is such a richly embroidered account of a young woman trying to make a life for herself in a foreign country and fighting for independence and friendship. This book isn't a romance in the same way that <i> Jane Eyre </i> is. I wasn't sure for a long time who the leading man would be (in fact he doesn't even appear until the second half of the book). And it isn't love at first sight, we watch it grow. <br /><br />I absolutely adored this book and it is now a firm favourtie of mine. I finished it last night and I finally closed the book in a daze. I don't want to give anything away, but I was <u> not </u> expecting what happended at the end at all. That came completely out of the blue for me. <br /><br />Go ahead, indluge and enjoy!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14442746731080880861" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_746291941" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/746291941" />
    <a title="Duane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24389390-duane"><img alt="Duane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403316391p2/24389390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/746291941?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Duane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Duane" href="/user/show/24389390-duane">Duane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=rated-books">rated-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=reviewed-books">reviewed-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=english-calssics">english-calssics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=guardian-1000">guardian-1000</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer746291941" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14916867027988743598">I liked this novel, I think partly because I pictured Charlotte as the character of Lucy Snowe. I felt like it was almost semi-autobiographical in nature. But it's still not in the same league with Jane Eyre, which will forever be considered Bronte's masterpiece. I read where George Eliot and Virginia Woolf believe Villette was her best novel. But in my opinion Jane Eyre is the gold standard of classic English literature. But still, I give Villette 4 stars, certainly worth reading.<br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_157390269" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/157390269" />
    <a title="Nancy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1690005-nancy"><img alt="Nancy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1245807338p2/1690005.jpg" /></a>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1690005-nancy?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1690005-nancy?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1690005-nancy?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer157390269" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16063240810870567636"> Villette lacks the fire and passion of Jane Eyre.<br/> Since we already know this is a fictionalized version of Charlotte Bronte's time in Brussels where she had some sort of relationship with the professor she worked for, this may be the reason for the tameness. <br/> There are many similarities in the characters of Jane Eyre and Lucy Snowe in that they are orphans, they are loners, they yearn for love and, for much of the book, they love from afar with no hope of reciprocation. Villette is a colder boo</span>
  <span id="freeText16063240810870567636" style="display:none"> Villette lacks the fire and passion of Jane Eyre.<br /> Since we already know this is a fictionalized version of Charlotte Bronte's time in Brussels where she had some sort of relationship with the professor she worked for, this may be the reason for the tameness. <br /> There are many similarities in the characters of Jane Eyre and Lucy Snowe in that they are orphans, they are loners, they yearn for love and, for much of the book, they love from afar with no hope of reciprocation. Villette is a colder book because I believe Charlotte Bronte was trying to put her real life love behind her by writing it out. I think it was done out of sadness, depression and loneliness and she built a wall between herself, her characters and her readers.<br /> <br /> There is such a thread of "this doesn't really matter" running through it that it is hard to become close to the characters or care very much about what happens to them. If they don't care, why should we?<br /><br /> Villette also lacks the pace of Jane Eyre and plods through dreary days with long, boring musings and moralizing. I got weary of the sermons. It was as if Bronte wrote anything that came into her mind, avoiding the crux of the situation. When in Brussels, she fell in love with a married man, had no hope of ever having a life with him and returned home to Yorkshire alone and miserable. Then she tried to write a book with a "so what" attitude and that didn't work for me.<br /> I just checked the reviews posted before mine, and feel like a salmon swimming upstream. Oh, well. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16063240810870567636" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText15528767628019802316" style="display:none">This was a really beautiful journey which often left me puzzled, but in the end I absolutely loved it. <div id="review_887055036" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/887055036" />
    <a title="Cindy Newton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6082517-cindy-newton"><img alt="Cindy Newton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432075684p2/6082517.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/887055036?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cindy Newton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cindy Newton" href="/user/show/6082517-cindy-newton">Cindy Newton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781593083168" href="/book/show/640052.Villette">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6082517-cindy-newton?shelf=owned-books">owned-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6082517-cindy-newton?shelf=classic-lit-english">classic-lit-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6082517-cindy-newton?shelf=b-n-classic">b-n-classic</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer887055036" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12950363264324089942">I'm not sure how to write a review for this book--I don't think I'm even qualified to. Yes, I read it, but not as well as it deserved. I went into it lightly, assuming that it was a weaker, watered-down, inferior version of <i>Jane Eyre</i>. By the end, I realized that this book is a force unto itself. The force of this book is subtle, though; it doesn't smack you between the eyes, but rather creeps up on you stealthily, winding almost invisible tentacles around your consciousness, catching you up into</span>
  <span id="freeText12950363264324089942" style="display:none">I'm not sure how to write a review for this book--I don't think I'm even qualified to. Yes, I read it, but not as well as it deserved. I went into it lightly, assuming that it was a weaker, watered-down, inferior version of <i>Jane Eyre</i>. By the end, I realized that this book is a force unto itself. The force of this book is subtle, though; it doesn't smack you between the eyes, but rather creeps up on you stealthily, winding almost invisible tentacles around your consciousness, catching you up into the story before you know you've been caught. Like its protagonist, Lucy Snowe, it lurks quietly, just watching; also like Lucy, the story has a hidden power.<br /><br />The story is the semi-autobiographical tale of Charlotte Bronte's unrequited love for her professor. The main character, Lucy Snowe, is an English orphan who flees England for the hope of adventure and a better life. She ends up in Villette, a fictional town that represents Brussels, where she takes a position in a girls' school as a teacher. She suffers an unrequited passion for one man, but ends by falling in love with another, who is ultimately a much better match for her.<br /><br />Lucy is telling the story, but we are still kept in the dark quite a bit as she proves to be an unreliable narrator. Her refusal to acknowledge certain truths about herself, even to herself, helps to keep her audience confused and mystified by events. <br /><br />All in all, I think this is a book that has hidden depths, and I feel that my own assumptions caused me to miss some of these layers of meaning. I need a re-read to really appreciate all that is there. When will that be? I have no idea, but I won't be able to do the book justice until then.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12950363264324089942" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2699474697" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2699474697" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/55478778-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1462205788p2/55478778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2699474697?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/55478778-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/12684498-villette">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55478778-laura?shelf=never-to-be-forgotten">never-to-be-forgotten</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55478778-laura?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55478778-laura?shelf=19th-century-women">19th-century-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55478778-laura?shelf=women-british">women-british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55478778-laura?shelf=dissenting-women">dissenting-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55478778-laura?shelf=reading-women-challenge">reading-women-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2699474697&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2699474697" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2655189987607053210">The genius of Charlotte Bronte is that she dissolves that thin line between reader and character. You dear reader feel everything as Lucy feels it. It is so painful to not know why M. Paul has offered no word of explanation for his absence. A brief note, saying 'Trust me' and then the fete night where Lucy's sensibilities are pushed beyond endurance and then the resolution.<br/><br/>And the final page - well - we are supposed to have learnt as Lucy has learnt, about Trust and Faith and Love. If you believ</span>
  <span id="freeText2655189987607053210" style="display:none">The genius of Charlotte Bronte is that she dissolves that thin line between reader and character. You dear reader feel everything as Lucy feels it. It is so painful to not know why M. Paul has offered no word of explanation for his absence. A brief note, saying 'Trust me' and then the fete night where Lucy's sensibilities are pushed beyond endurance and then the resolution.<br /><br />And the final page - well - we are supposed to have learnt as Lucy has learnt, about Trust and Faith and Love. If you believe in love then M. Paul will be returned safely.<br /><br />What more to say.<br /><br />Actually I decided there is quite a lot more to say - reading Villette, and then attempting to respond to the whole, required me to consider - what exactly is the nature of a review?<br /><br />I think the basic response - is that I want everyone else to read and enjoy the book as much as I did, and then to contact me and tell me all the pieces they loved, hated, wanted to change, couldn't improve upon, how it related to them etc. etc. etc.<br /> <br />If my followers experience different emotions, a different rationale then no problem - the point is to read and appreciate her, and also I think because she's one of the Very Best. The experience of reading Villette will ultimately give you a more referenced and perceptive vision from which to read other novels - be it from the 19th, 20th or 21st century, and certainly you can go back in time to - when the Novel begins - mid 18th century? Tristram Shandy - by Laurence Sterne. Any suggestions folks?<br /><br />So to Villette - Bronte is an intellectual writer - and she writes in the style of her contemporaries which require that your abilities as writer be constantly reaffirmed. To this end, the text is full of references - to the Bible, Old and New, Greek philosophers, contemporary, and notable greats, English History - and surprisingly to Thomas Hardy's <i>Tess of the D'Urbervilles</i> published in 1891. If you are not familiar with these noteworthy tomes and other writers - then yes it can be off putting - but we have the esteemed Tony Tanner, (a Fellow of King's College Cambridge, and Reader) who provides the End Notes and an Introduction. Can anyone explain the Tess reference, considering Hardy didn't create her until - 38 years <i>after</i> Villette? <br /><br />Nevermind - the esteemed Tanner also translates the heavy use of conversational French - although I generally preferred my own, and only used his when I struggled with vocab. All of this can be a little intimidating - Do not be put off. Ms Bronte applied mid-19th century writerly style, mixed with her own and being a woman, she needed to establish her credentials not to mention maintain her assumed status as Currer Bell.<br /><br />If you're a Bible reader then you will love Bronte's references to the Holy Works - try this:<br /><br />Chapter 19: Note 8. (p.280) <i>Nebuchadnezzar's hottest furnace</i>: the miraculous deliverance of three Israelites from one of these furnaces, whither they had been despatched by King Nebuchadnezzar of Babylon (for refusing to worship a golden image), converted the king forwith to Judaism. See Daniel, iii.<br /><br />The note refers to words spoken by M. Paul to Lucy - a comment on her integrity of character.<br /><br />Or try this: <br /> <br />Chapter 25: Note 4. (p.370) <i>too solid flesh:</i> the phrase is from the first line of Hamlet's first soliloquy:<br /> O! that this too solid flesh would melt,<br /> Thaw and resolve itself into a dew;<br /> (I, ii 129-30)<br /><br />This is Paulina referring to her dear Papa - M. Home de Bassompierre as being immutable. He is an intractable character unwilling to change his mind - a good Scotsman in my opinion - one of the few characters - who quickly and easily sums up Lucy's position. Which is what he does when Paulina asks Lucy what she does - Lucy replies: 'I am a teacher,'...<br /><br />Our narrator provides a careful lead in, culminating in the Count's reply:<br /><br /><i>He did not know much about Lucy Snowe: what he knew, he did not very accurately comprehend: indeed his misconceptions of my character often made me smile; but he saw my walk in life lay rather on the shady side of the hill; he gave me credit for doing my endeavour to keep the course honestly straight; he would have helped me if he could: having no opportunity of helping he still wished me well. When he did look at me, his eye was kind; when he did speak, his voice was benevolent.<br /> 'Yours,' said he, 'is an arduous calling. I wish you strength and health to win in it - success.'<br /> <br /> His fair little daughter did not take the information quite so composedly: she fixed on me a pair of eyes wide with wonder - almost with dismay.<br /> 'Are you a teacher?' cried she. Then having paused on the unpalatable idea,...<br /> 'And why do you go on with it?'<br /> Her father looked at, and I feared, was going to check her; but he only said, 'Proceed Polly ... Well Miss Snowe, why do you go on with it?'<br /> 'Chiefly, I fear, for the sake of the money I get.'</i><br /><br /><br />Actually in this tiny section - there is the whole plot - Lucy's struggle to establish herself as a person equal to those around her - and when I use the word equal - Bronte and I share terms - we mean equal in character, intellect, ability, morality. <br /><br />But I would also like to point out - how simple is her style when - it comes to the crunch. There are plenty of examples of what I would call highfalutin language - but we'll skip those.<br /><br />Let's focus on the plot. Here is the point at which she must return to the Rue Fossette to Madam Beck's Pensionnat and her work as a teacher. Dr John/Graham has promised to write to her and to maintain his family's relationship with her.<br /><br /><i>'And will Graham really write?' I questioned, as I sank tired on the edge of the bed.<br /> Reason, coming stealthily up to me through the twilight of that long, dim chamber, whispered sedately, -<br /> 'He may write once. So kind is his nature, it may stimulate him for once to make the effort.</i> <br /><br />Lucy has allowed herself to hope that there will be a continued interest in herself and her circumstances from Dr John, and indeed his mother Mrs Bretton, who is in fact Lucy's Godmother, and her only semblance of family.<br />The conversation between Lucy and Reason continues for several pages, she wants to write to Dr John, allow him to see the real her, but Reason answers:<br /><br /><i>At your peril you cherish that idea, or suffer its influence to animate any writing of yours!'<br /> 'But if I feel, may I <b>never</b> express?'<br /> '<b>Never</b>! declared Reason.</i><br /><br />At the end of this bitter argument - with herself, Lucy is found in the classe, huddled by the stove trying to imbibe some warmth into her frozen mind and body - and interesting it is M. Paul who finds her thus:<br /><br /><i>'Mademoiselle, vous etes triste.'<br /> 'Monsieur, j'en ai bien le droit.'<br /> 'Vous etes malade de coeur et d'humeur,' he pursued. 'You are at once mourneful and mutinous. I see on your cheek two tears which I know are hot as two sparks, and salt as two crystals of the sea. While I speak you eye me strangely. Shall I tell you of what I am reminded while watching you?'<br /> 'Monsieur, I shall be called away to prayers shortly; my time for conversation is very scant and brief at this hour - excuse -'<br /> 'I excuse everything,' he interrrupted; 'my mood is so meek, neither rebuff nor, perhaps, insult could ruffle it. You remind me, then, of a young she wild creature, new caught, untamed, viewing with a mixture of fire and fear the first entrance of the breaker-in.'</i><br /><br />And he means 'breaker-in' to her heart not something else, automatically considered by modern readers.<br /><br />Our narrator often describes M. Paul as gnome like - but here the sense of his ability to discern exactly what she is feeling borders on the other-wordly - a true elf, gnome, magi - he is not privy to Lucy's past or recent excursion to La Terrasse the comfortable home of Graham and Mrs Bretton, and yet he reads Lucy absolutely.<br /><br />Which woman - out there has not yearned for a man -or mortal to address her so? To observe with the naked eye and yet see beyond what is on the surface? This is extraordinarily racy for present times - more so for 1853 - and yet M. Paul is no demon lover or celibate priest or any other incarnation he could be - but simply a man, aware of Lucy struggling to retain all her desires beneath a smooth facade. All the normal desires of a young woman, to have friendship, love, security, status, comforts - a pleasant life, with excitements as well as work and struggles.<br /><br />As M. Paul identifies after Lucy's performance in the school play "vous etes une petite ambitiouse". Yes - she is clever. She has desires and aims, but are these Needs any different from any woman young or old in any time period?<br /><br />Lucy's particular historical time setting means - she cannot advance in society through work or employment. In the early 19th century, her only way forward is to marry. <br /><br />This is the essential difference therefore, between then and now - her options are extremely narrow, but she battles on. Severely tried, she makes her way but will not use the wiles of certain ones - such as Ginevra Fanshaw. Nor does she have the fine breeding and background of Paulina - or her looks. Lucy's only advantages are what lie internally - her morals and intellect, her character - and it is on these that Charlotte Bronte builds her novel. <br /><br />Lucy chooses to believe, no matter how arduous the journey that - through her own strength and determination she will find a measure of happiness and success - and indeed she does.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2655189987607053210" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1648865320" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1648865320" />
    <a title="Amanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7897151-amanda"><img alt="Amanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461450073p2/7897151.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1648865320?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda" href="/user/show/7897151-amanda">Amanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780062356161" href="/book/show/23460962-villette">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7897151-amanda?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1648865320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12588050039063847171">10/26/16 "Forget the modern debate over 'likeable' female characters - Lucy is prickly, repressed, untrustworthy, unattractive, judgmental, in constant denial of her own feelings, desperate for affection, violently anti-Catholic - in short an IMPOSSIBLE female character. There are even times when not only Lucy but Bronte herself hides significant information about the other characters from the readers, often casually mentioning having withheld it long after the fact. She is difficult to sympathi</span>
  <span id="freeText12588050039063847171" style="display:none">10/26/16 "Forget the modern debate over 'likeable' female characters - Lucy is prickly, repressed, untrustworthy, unattractive, judgmental, in constant denial of her own feelings, desperate for affection, violently anti-Catholic - in short an IMPOSSIBLE female character. There are even times when not only Lucy but Bronte herself hides significant information about the other characters from the readers, often casually mentioning having withheld it long after the fact. She is difficult to sympathize with, because she does not seek to be understood, not by us nor by anyone else. She seeks always to appear smaller, not because she enjoys being ignored, because she has found being human-sized altogether too painful to endure. She has no hope of power of pleasing, but this does not mean she has stopped wishing that she had it." -from Mallory Ortberg's introduction <br /><br />I had a difficult time reading Villette. The first few chapters and the protagonist, Lucy Snowe, never really captured and held my attention. I ended up finishing it by listening to the audiobook and reading along. But then the ending blindsided me, and the introduction, which I read afterwards, made me cry.<br /><br />I recall my great love for Jane Eyre happened after I reread it. And I feel my love for Villette can only grow. Lucy and Jane are very much alike, but Lucy is far more real than Jane. Lucy is solely concerned with survival and often succumbs to despair. She only rarely allows herself to daydream about the thing Jane craves: love. Lucy never asks for love; she has only known grief and does not want to go through another unnecessary bout caused by the loss of it. I very much see parts of myself in Lucy and feel very kindred to her, especially since my days far more closely resemble Lucy's than they will ever Jane's, with all their excitement and promises.<br /><br />Lucy's story doesn't reward you for listening to it, but there are rewards to be found in listening to Lucy Snowe.<br /><br /><br />8/25/17 For the most part, this rereading experience was not very enjoyable. I think, on the whole, I will always find the first part of Villette a slog to overcome. There is a lot of the French language and many new, unlikable characters to meet and hear about, and many unsettling settings to discover. During the first part, too, we barely get a chance to understand Lucy, who she is, how she functions. She makes an effort to hide herself from the reader.<br /><br />During the rereading, I saw through a few things that Lucy intended to hide from the reader, and I looked on a couple male characters differently - oppositely - than I did when reading it for the first time. I enjoyed knowing I knew Lucy better than she perhaps wanted me to. Overall, however, Villette is not a happy book. Lucy is not a happy person and her life is not easy or full of pleasurable things. She's an outsider most of the time and is rarely, truly a part of someone else's life. This is a book about the pains of solitude, unrequited love and unfulfilled love. It is full of unwelcoming characters and places. It is a story that desperately tries to keep you at arm's length.<br /><br />But after all that, Lucy feels like a friend.<br /><br />One day this will get a true 5 stars from me - when I find enjoyment in the first part of the novel. Until then I will continue to reread Villette and never forget Lucy Snowe.<br /><br /><br />7/14/19 this was a more enjoyable reading experience, though I’ve dropped my rating to three stars. This novel continues to have too many unlikeable characters for me to love and a plot that feels pantsed more than planned. I always enjoy CB’s writing, though this book has some of her most complicated language and extended metaphors that can be confusing. I enjoyed seeing the small details in the text and making connections I hadn’t before. But this is probably my least favorite of her works, though I still find merit in it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12588050039063847171" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Elham" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43665577-elham"><img alt="Elham" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585488816p2/43665577.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Elham by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43665577-elham?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43665577-elham?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43665577-elham?shelf=women-s-writings">women-s-writings</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1344396219" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1659752255605349259">This book is dark, dark; even darker than any existentialist novel I have ever read, and how true and realistic. It seems that this novel is a kind of semi-autobiography.<br/><br/>Like Jane Eyre, this time also the book starts with the stories of a girl, Lucy Snow, living for a while with her godmother. But it was only for a short while. Then she grows up (we don't know anything about the years in between from her 14-23 – we just know that she had a difficult life that she had to work and nurse an old wom</span>
  <span id="freeText1659752255605349259" style="display:none">This book is dark, dark; even darker than any existentialist novel I have ever read, and how true and realistic. It seems that this novel is a kind of semi-autobiography.<br /><br />Like Jane Eyre, this time also the book starts with the stories of a girl, Lucy Snow, living for a while with her godmother. But it was only for a short while. Then she grows up (we don't know anything about the years in between from her 14-23 – we just know that she had a difficult life that she had to work and nurse an old woman) she travels to another city, Villette, alone, where they speak French. She becomes an English teacher.<br /><br />But it was not as easy as those summary lines. She is alone, no family, no money. She has to live on her own feet; she must work not for humanity and helping other people, not morality…to have a roof above her head.<br /><br />What makes this book dark?? Loneliness, solitude, broken heart and the price of freedom. The freedom of expressing yourself, the freedom of being your own self, not just putting a mask on your face, a mask of a "beautiful woman" , pretend to be a lover and always remain a dependent creature. Independence above all...Independence is necessary, but this is suffering. How can you be "a woman of intellect" and still be loved?? And perhaps if you had a wealthy father you could have the chance of seducing men who besides beauty (a most essential factor) they would die for your money too.<br /><br />You can't breathe in a place where you only have to work, where they steal love from you, it is a prison…you need a change, you are strong. <br /><br />This book is amazing. Charlotte Brontë is amazing and very courageous to write such book in her time.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1659752255605349259" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer321968933808439597">(edited this with some expanding thoughts:) The story of a woman half-forced to indenpendence, having to find her way in a foreign, largely Catholic country; to find a satisfying job and perhaps love. It's not a straight, clear road that she might've hoped for, but something that makes her grow <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[into mature, independent stability that is not without implied (or clear, if you view it so) tragedy <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br/><br/>One has to remember while reading this that certain prejudices of Cathol</span>
  <span id="freeText321968933808439597" style="display:none">(edited this with some expanding thoughts:) The story of a woman half-forced to indenpendence, having to find her way in a foreign, largely Catholic country; to find a satisfying job and perhaps love. It's not a straight, clear road that she might've hoped for, but something that makes her grow <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[into mature, independent stability that is not without implied (or clear, if you view it so) tragedy <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />One has to remember while reading this that certain prejudices of Catholic religion from a British protestant point of view appear in the text, but it's not so heavy that I couldn't finish it, and it's somewhat in the background. This book shows also yet again what a great mind (and later what a great loss we had) in Charlotte Bronte.<br /><br />Reading this I found myself rooting for her survival, her independence and stability of mind (there's one rather hallucinatory scene in one chapter). I also found the writing tight (no feel of 'needs to be shorter') yet with great moods. I enjoyed this book all the way, and will read it again. Recommended.</span>

<div id="review_61982742" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61982742" />
    <a title="Captain Sir Roddy, R.N. (Ret.)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret"><img alt="Captain Sir Roddy, R.N. (Ret.)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453308913p2/2482609.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61982742?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Captain Sir Roddy, R.N. (Ret.)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Captain Sir Roddy, R.N. (Ret.)" href="/user/show/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret">Captain Sir Roddy, R.N. (Ret.)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780679409885" href="/book/show/1831331.Villette">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61982742" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4675858481150953869">I cry in anguish, "Oh <i>Villette</i>, <i>Villette</i>, <i>Villette</i>!"<br/><br/>It was a feeling that came upon me as I read this novel; the palpable feeling of—<br/><br/>The cold grey storms of the fall and winter, the relentless building winds, the rain pounding against the window—those dark and dreary days of loneliness—all of the losses have brought you a smothering and almost overwhelming mantle of grief. You see, and write of, the Love around you, but feel the throbbing ache, day after day, night after night, of never receivin</span>
  <span id="freeText4675858481150953869" style="display:none">I cry in anguish, "Oh <i>Villette</i>, <i>Villette</i>, <i>Villette</i>!"<br /><br />It was a feeling that came upon me as I read this novel; the palpable feeling of—<br /><br />The cold grey storms of the fall and winter, the relentless building winds, the rain pounding against the window—those dark and dreary days of loneliness—all of the losses have brought you a smothering and almost overwhelming mantle of grief. You see, and write of, the Love around you, but feel the throbbing ache, day after day, night after night, of never receiving Love in return.<br /><br />I lost count of the tears that fell as I read your account, Miss Lucy Snowe; or, should I call you, Miss Charlotte?<br /><br />This novel, this <i>Villette</i>, like an arrow fletched fair, flew true, oh so true, and pierced your beating heart; and from that mortal wound poured the secrets of your soul, your inner-most being; laid bare for all to see. The incalculable loss of your older sisters, then Branwell, your dearest Emily, and finally quiet little Anne. This towering testament to loneliness, to sorrow, swept me, your Reader, relentlessly through the unimagined torrent of your human emotions—your grief, your fears, your reserved passion, your quiet grace, steadfast loyalty, and your resolute strength and faith.<br /><br />I felt guilty as I read, Little Woman, looking over my shoulder at every pause; afraid that you should find me picking the lock of your secret diary; spellbound as I turned the pages, one after the other, reading your most intimate, personal, and painful thoughts and the passionate feelings that poured forth onto the page. Intensely captivated by the dialog between your Passion and your Reason, the conversations between your Imagination and your Matter; but I read on. Until it became too much; I averted my eyes, and I wept.<br /><br />As I sit here, writing these words, I am absolutely overwhelmed. I don’t know that I have ever read a book that has moved me quite like Charlotte Bronte’s final novel, <i>Villette</i>. A timeless and moving experience from its first words, to its final “Farewell.” I am without words, Little Woman. I know this though, Miss Lucy Snowe, Miss Charlotte Bronte, I shall Love you always.<br /><br />In tribute to the commitment you made to all who have read, or will read, this personal ‘Testament’ of yours over the ages, may your own words prove prophetic—<br /><br /><blockquote>“Proof of a life to come must be given. In fire and in blood do we trace the record throughout nature. In fire and in blood does it cross our own experience. Sufferer, faint not through terror of this burning evidence. Tired wayfarer, gird up thy loins, look upward, march onward. Pilgrims and brother mourners, join in friendly company. Dark through the wilderness of this world stretches the way for most of us; equal and steady be our tread; be our cross our banner. For staff we have His promise, whose ‘word is tried, whose way is perfect:’ for present hope His providence, ‘who gives the shield of salvation, whose gentleness makes great;’ for final home His bosom, who ‘dwells in the height of Heaven;’ for crowning prize a glory, exceeding and eternal.”</blockquote><br /><br />Farewell, Little Woman, fare thee well.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4675858481150953869" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1893701606" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1893701606" />
    <a title="Jess" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41763158-jess"><img alt="Jess" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525599242p2/41763158.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1893701606?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jess" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jess" href="/user/show/41763158-jess">Jess</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141199887" href="/book/show/15990651-villette">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41763158-jess?shelf=cover-envy">cover-envy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41763158-jess?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41763158-jess?shelf=feminism-done-properly">feminism-done-properly</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41763158-jess?shelf=insufferable-main-characters">insufferable-main-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41763158-jess?shelf=you-n-me-could-write-a-bad-romance">you-n-me-could-write-a-bad-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41763158-jess?shelf=re-reads">re-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41763158-jess?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41763158-jess?shelf=bronte">bronte</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41763158-jess?shelf=2019">2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1893701606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17864465848314227461"><i>"You are good, you are beautiful, but you are not mine."</i><br/><br/>Bravissima, Brontë! <br/><br/><i>Villette</i>: melancholy, bitter, remorseless and much less urgent than <i>Jane Eyre</i>. And yet, it is a far more nuanced, accomplished, complex and mesmerising work than its older sibling.<br/><br/>Charlotte Brontë's final novel is an exquisite examination of loneliness and unrequited love. It’s a deeply interior novel and whilst the paucity of action might act as a deterrent to some, the dark introspection of Lucy Snowe and her existence</span>
  <span id="freeText17864465848314227461" style="display:none"><i>"You are good, you are beautiful, but you are not mine."</i><br /><br />Bravissima, Brontë! <br /><br /><i>Villette</i>: melancholy, bitter, remorseless and much less urgent than <i>Jane Eyre</i>. And yet, it is a far more nuanced, accomplished, complex and mesmerising work than its older sibling.<br /><br />Charlotte Brontë's final novel is an exquisite examination of loneliness and unrequited love. It’s a deeply interior novel and whilst the paucity of action might act as a deterrent to some, the dark introspection of Lucy Snowe and her existence on the periphery of society is endlessly fascinating. Actually, Lucy Snowe herself might act as a deterrent; she’s watchful and malicious, emotionally unavailable, closed and inherently unknowable. She’s notorious for her unreliability, snobbery, reticence about her personal circumstances and, quite frankly, her <i>miserable</i> outlook on life. But sometimes impassive can be mistaken for passive: <i>Villette</i> is at its heart a story about how to be an independent chick in a world that doesn’t necessarily want you. And as for Lucy’s reservation, let’s just say that it makes <i>Villette</i> very, very unpredictable.<br /><br />This is perhaps most well known for being Brontë’s most autobiographical work, drawing on her experiences as a student and then a teacher in Brussels, as well as her disturbing fascination with her (married) Belgian tutor, Monsieur Heger. It is also widely acknowledged as her first <s>attempt at a</s> novel, <i>The Professor</i>, with a serious revamp. Whilst Charlotte’s violent religious intolerance and devout physiognomist status make another appearance here, what sets <i>Villette</i> apart for me is its powerful depiction of mental health. To a modern reader, it becomes painfully apparent that Lucy Snowe suffers from clinical depression, or what she perfectly describes as a “<i>sorrowful indifference to existence... - a despairing resignation to reach betimes the end of all things earthly.</i>” Although this unique insight into a Victorian perspective of neurosis is especially interesting, Brontë’s presentation of the experience is startlingly modern. (Also, <i>Villette</i> is worth the read just for the brilliantly psychedelic episode where Lucy gets high on opium. I rest my case.)<br /><br />As usual, the prose is beautiful - if a little lacking in economy and saturated with French and bizarre imagery, as Charlotte Brontë is often wont to do. Her descriptions of setting in particular are absolutely <i>exquisite</i> - this description of a chandelier in a theatre left me breathless:<br /><br /><i>"Pendant from the dome, flamed a mass that dazzled me - a mass, I thought, of rock-crystal, sparkling with facets, streaming with drops, ablaze with stars, and gorgeously tinged with dews of gems dissolved, or fragments of rainbows shivered."</i><br /><br />The narrative itself gets off to a rocky and rather uninspiring start, but the seemingly irrelevant observations of the early chapters are crucial to the intricate framework of the novel. <i>Villette</i> in many areas does not feel as though it is Lucy's story: she narrates the lives of secondary characters with acute detail and perception whilst withholding information about her own. She's watchful; disturbingly so. <br /><br />The romances/passions is where the novel falls short for me - Lucy's emotional torture is almost unbearable to read, primarily because she never <i>allows</i> herself to acknowledge her feelings. In this facet, the novel is intrinsically unsatisfying. We all know that Charlotte had a bit of a soft spot for capricious and brooding Byronic heroes, but one character in particular takes the biscuit. Lucy Snowe genuinely falls for two very different guys, and whilst one is not entirely undeserving of that affection, the other most certainly is. Without naming names, this particular character is choleric, tyrannical and not entirely fanciable, to be honest <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Monsieur Paul <i>hits</i> Madame Beck, for God’s sake! He frequently humiliates Lucy and attempts to control /censor what she consumes eg. the Cleopatra and tearing pages out of her books. Well, if that's what you're into... <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. I appreciate that Brontë orchestrated the ‘romance’ to progress subtly, but the relationship is unhealthy and the epitome of emotional dependency. No, just no. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Although Charlotte does appropriately kill him off before Lucy could make the mistake of marrying him, so maybe she did learn from Anne?!! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />Despite its frustrations, <i>Villette</i> is a wonderful, wonderful novel; so intricately plotted and layered with such profound observation. Ultimately though, I couldn’t fully emotionally commit myself because the recipients of Lucy’s love were not worthy of it. If you decide to give Brontë 's grand finale a go, don't expect <i>Jane Eyre</i>, because you ain't gonna get it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17864465848314227461" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
